### Woshington Pravda cries for H1b's

- **From:** kiyoinc@ibm.XOUT.net (cory hamasaki)
- **Date:** 2000-09-16T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,alt.computer.consultants,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost>`

```
Front Page - September 16, 2000, if you're not in DeeCee hit 
http://www.washingtonpost.com, it'll be online for 2 weeks.

Poor, poor H1b's.  They were tricked into coming to the U.S. to take 
$100,000/year jobs for a max of 6 years.  Big cars, fancy houses, 
cheap gasoline, a luxurious lifestyle, all detailed in this remarkable
story.  Some thought H1b meant junior-green-card. 

Tricked! Conned to be slave labor at IBM, Apple, HP, Microsoft for 
big, big bucks but with a time limit of 6 years.  

Now that their time is up, they're being abandoned by the system.  
Some are making plans to return home, fat wads of hundred dollar bills
in their pockets, but they're crying because they'll miss their 
friends, the season premier of Buffy the Vampire Slayer, and other 
life essentials.

But hey, Y2K remediation is over.  We don't need them to turn the 
crank on the code-mills.  If they're doing web-work, they can do that 
just fine back home. 

Y2K is over, it's time to cancel the H1b program entirely.   Let the 
free market set rates for Captain COBOL.
```

```
Here's some quality whining from the article:

=============start
"Who, who will help me?" Sathya pleads in a rare burst of self-pity. "My
latte-drinking
lawyer? He already moved on to the next Indian name. My company? They 
dropped me like a
hot potato. The government? Who am I to them?

"It's shameful that this country can't deliver what it promised."
=============end

This is what he's crying about:

=============start
Now there were more plans. With two salaries totaling six figures, they 
bought a house, cream
and brick in a tasteful development called Huntington Village. New, 
nothing used, just like the
rest on the block. But they picked this particular one because the 
school bus stopped right at
the mailbox.

Two years later, and Sathya himself had changed. Not just because he 
watched "Ally McBeal"
and "Monday Night Football," or because he was setting up "play dates" 
for his daughter.
=============end

This guy made good money for 6 years, enough that if he had saved,
invested, he will live like a king in his homeland.  Even on *one* 
year's savings, twenty-thirty grand, he could live well.

What's so wrong with going home.

Heck, with 6 years savings and the proceeds from the sale of a 
house, he could set up a terrific business in India or just retire to an
estate, never have to work again.  He's smart, educated, experienced,
bi-lingual at least, should have a financial stake such as 99.99 % of 
his countrymen could only dream of.

  "Poooor, poooor, pitiful H-1-bee
   poor, poor, pitiful H-1-beeeee"

Now that Y2K remediation is over (if it is), he can apply his software 
skills to building a product of his own.  Why do it in San Francisco, 
Boston, or Chicago when all a programmer needs is a laptop and and his 
mind.

He can sit at home, visit his family and friends, have servants prepare 
his meals, clean his house, and write the next killer application.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Woshington Pravda cries for H1b's

- **From:** Catkiller <noneof@urbiz>
- **Date:** 2000-09-19T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,alt.computer.consultants,comp.lang.cobol

```
This H1B is a lot of horse-pucky. 
Last two years I worked contracts as a consultant at two very large
firms. The crazy thing was that with y2k staring them down the throat
they started terminating their sys progs and some app progs who had
grown up with the systems. In the other shop they swapped out
mainframes 2 months before the rollover and also chased away 
the main sys pgmer by chanting " out source, out source" over and over
til the guy got a clue and left for a job elsewhere.

I really didn't like having to act in some capacity as a scab so to
speak and each time major decisions were made I deferred to the
sys prog who was now consulting at the same firm parttime since he was
the only one who knew the system innards. Duhhhhh. When there was too
much overtime I let him have all he could handle. 

Meanwhile guess what ? There were scads of rajahs and rajahettes
oozing  around the cubicles. Doing absolutely zilch except using magic
markers to paint little red dots on their foreheads. 

You can stick the H1B and green cards up your wahzooo. Get a clue ,
clueless one , the big firms are still downsizing out programmers and
headcount.  What do they then do? Bring in the rajahs. 

I am glad to be back out of that market area and in the small scale
arena again. At least they appreciate what you do. The pay isn't so
great but the respect is worthwhile. Big companies suck big time.
```

```
"Catkiller" <noneof@urbiz> wrote in message

…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> headcount.  What do they then do? Bring in the rajahs.

I have basically tried to stay out of this thread, but I cannot help it, but
butting in. I am amazed of what level of "understanding" and "insight" some
people tend to show. Just by the use of your arguments here, you are not
only stating that you don't have a single clue of what this is about, but
you also uncover the real reason for your bashing against the H1-B visas,
being a racist.

You go ahead be what you are, but remember, you can never be respected for
your opinion, when you carry arguments like that.

And of course, now you think that I am myself black as the night, with
colorful spots all over my body, washing once a year and what ever else
negative ideas you got.

Well, I am almost as white as the clouds on the sky, I am a programmer, I
work for money (gee!), I am Norwegian (Norway is a country in northern
Europe in case you did not know, and it is one of the wealthies countries in
the world), and guess what! I am also one of those horrible, nasty dirt bags
that with a H1-B visa in my hand steal jobs from good Americans, "I do their
work for nothing and I sneak around the cubicles doing nothing but painting
dots in my forehead".

Now, when this is said, I have read the article in Washington Post, and I
must confess, I have no empathy for the people interviewed their. I can
understand that in their own misery and failure of realizing that they took
a chance and failed, they turn around and use whatever lies and imaginations
that come in their way in a pathetic attempt to raise sympathy and
understanding for their situation. H1-B was never intended the way they have
expressed this in the article, and I am somewhat surprised that such a big
newspaper as the Washington Post let this pass by without comment, but I
guess that is what the whole thing is about, making a story that sells.

H1-B is for three years, almost automatically extendable for another three
years, to target a short time need and nothing else, they might not have
known that back in India, but being in the US, studying here for years, and
working here with a H1-B, they should have known.

So, the misery they are into, is their own, and nothing else, but they are
people like anyone else. When we fail, we don't like to accept the failure,
but try to find someone / something else to nail it to. Just like you
apparently are failing your career for some reason and find it convenient to
bash Indians which are here on a H1-B working permit. It is more comfortable
that way.

The funny thing is, almost all of the current inhabitants of the United
States are themselves either immigrants or anchestors to immigrants, (not
many natives left, is it?), so how come this anger and fear for people "like
them selves"? I do of course understand and support that the US (like any
country) cannot let in everybody in the world, there are social, demographic
and economical reasons for limiting immigration, although, when one is
emotionally affected by the consequences it probably looks like an all cruel
world out there.

By the way, what is the problem with Canada? I have never been there but
from what I have seen on television and pictures, it looks like a beautiful
country, and the article presents it as it should be next to Hell. If I was
a Canadian, I would have felt insulted by such an article. From how I
understand the article, they are free to stay in Canada, well, why not
establish there then?

Cheesle
```

```
Oh here is the H1-B poll:

IEEE-USA/Harris Poll, Sept. 1998: (national sample of 1,000 adults)
Question: Do you favor or oppose Congress "allowing U.S. companies to sponsor
190,000 additional foreign technical workers as temporary employees for up to six years?"
Response: Favor -- 16 percent;  Oppose -- 82 percent;  Unsure -- 2 percent.

Question: Do you agree or disagree that "lower wages paid to temporary foreign
workers harm U.S. professional wages?"
Response: Strongly or mostly agree -- 75 percent;  Mostly or strongly disagree -- 23
percent;  Unsure --  percent.

Question: Do you agree or disagree that "allowing companies to hire additional
temporary foreign professionals reduces employment opportunities for U.S. technical
workers?"
Response: Strongly or mostly agree -- 86 percent;  Mostly or strongly disagree -- 13
percent:  Unsure -- 1 percent.

Question: Do you agree or disagree that "without adding additional temporary foreign
workers the United States might be forced to transfer work overseas?"
Response: Strongly or mostly agree -- 31 percent;  Mostly or strongly disagree -- 66
percent;  Unsure -- 3 percent.
(Source: Institute of Electrical and Electronics Engineers-USA press release,

September 16, 1988 -- connect to press release and POLL CHARTS)
```

```
I think this is taking a simplistic view of things. In any
market, increasing the supply even of low quality goods/services
reduces prices right up the quality chain, by flow on effects.
Why hire a good programmer when you can get a mediocre one cheap? 

Of course you're probably still better to hire the good
programmer depending on the skill level required, but even if it
doesn't make sense to hire the poor programmer, his existence
still forces doen prices.

In any case, I have met some pretty smart Indians, so the
assumption of poor quality is not necessarilyy justified... when
a person has a poor command of a language and social mores in a
new country they sound a lot less smart than they really are.

Such as, in the USA it is essential to go around saying how great
you are all the time or people think you are useless. In most
other parts of the world this is regarded as arrogance and hubris
or at least tasteless behaviour.

Tim Josling
```

```
seemingly no one to hire, even if you pay as much as $50K a year!

it may. Or course, all of this applies only if there are fact is any
```

```
So if salaries have more than doubled, and costs have doubled, I make more, not
less.  It doesn't matter which parts of my expenses are above inflation and which are
below - it is my total expenditures (inflation), which effect my spending money.  And
I certainly have more spending money now than I did then - of course I also have 20
years more experience, so a better test is whether young programmers have more
spending money than I had, without putting up with less desirable houses.  My
observations are that they have more than I had.  Maybe only morons measure income by
```

```
Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote in message
news:39C7CBF8.48CDD91B@brazee.net...
> > Then it is time for the price of produce to adjust to

> > producing it paying a decent wage and benefits. If
produce growers
> > go out of business, so be it.  If we have to slap on
some tariffs,
> > so be it. Its totally fucked importing third world
workers and then
> > giving them amnesty every few years. Why do I want to
butt-fuck this
> > country just to pay 1/2 price (or whatever) on a
motherfucking
> > head of lettuce.
>
> I don't see the relationship.  How does it hurt our
country to allow our farmers to
> hire labor instead of going out of business

Some might go out of business, but generally speaking, food
demand is relatively inelastic.  If prices rise, people pay
the higher price, and the farmers don't go out of business.
It's like the minimum wage.  Every time it comes up for a
vote, the business community complains that it will put
small businesses under (like an extra $10 a day will put the
local convenience store out of business!), but in reality,
all it does is get more money circulating in the community,
and increases the local wealth.

By encouraging labor at lower rates, you are making life
better for people who have servants and employees, but you
are hurting the overall economy.  The difference between the
US economy and the Mexican economy is that in the US we have
minimum wage laws that result in working people having money
to spend.  If we get in a race to the bottom with the Third
World, we'll all go down the tubes.






>
> > > Employers need to find their most loyal employees and
train them.  And then
> > > provide non monetary benefits to keep them happy.
(money helps too, but not as
> > > much as other, harder to match benefits).

I agree.  Most of the people in senior positions in the
computer industry did not study computer science when they
were in school because it wasn't available.  This is a
totally bogus argument, about needing to find people with BS
degrees in Computer Science.
We don't need and extra 300,000 people a year doing
high-tech research.

I disagree with you on the money issue, though.  With the
cost of housing, medical care, and college, most people are
not really making ends meet unless they are pulling in at
least $75K, and most of the people who are going to be
trained are probably making $35-$45, which seems to be the
market value of non-techie skilled people.

One of the reasons businesses don't like to train their
dependable, loyal, responsible, hard-working staff is that
once they have computer skills, those employees are now
worth a lot more on the open market, yet because of the
nature of American businesses, they are restricted to giving
their staff a 6%, or some other really dorky raise, and it's
too easy for the now-valuable employee to leave.

We will have get businesses used to the fact that if the
employee is now worth $62K, they should get it, even if they
were only making $38K last year.
```

```
No, the difference is that we have a demand for workers which is such
that very few people earn the minimum wage.  You won't even find minimum
wage workers at McDonalds.  We need workers, and are willing to pay for
them at a level higher than minimum wages.

Now, Northern Mexico is providing jobs, and people jump for the chance
of having those low-paying jobs to put food on their tables.  And with
the exception of the mining industry, every time industrialization has
provided these jobs (in a free economy), the jobs have been followed by
significant increase of real wealth for the workers within a generation,
as they gain skills and their employers leverage those skills into more
wealth for them (and incidentally for the workers).
```

```
Dio <Dio@dio.com> wrote in message

are
> > not really making ends meet unless they are pulling in

> > least $75K,

that, you would
> be forced to conclude that the overwhelming majority of
Americans are
> not making ends meet.

Which is why so many families need two wage earners.  And
why single-parent families have such a difficult time.
It wasn't that long ago that most families did just fine
with one wage earner.


>    Exactly how do you determine what an employee is really
worth?

One way is to decide if you'd make more money hiring the
person than not.
If you are hiring an employee so you can make more money,
you decide if you'd make more money with the employee at
that wage level, or not.

If you are hiring an employee to free up your time, you
decide if you'd do the work for the wages. If not, then the
time you save by not having to do the work is of more value
than the money.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Woshington Pravda cries for H1b's

_(reply depth: 15)_

- **From:** Cranky Young Half-Irishman <coim@ireland.net>
- **Date:** 2000-09-23T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,alt.computer.consultants,comp.lang.cobol,us.issues.occupations.computer-programmers
- **Message-ID:** `<39CD0CFB.164C6221@ireland.net>`
- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <39CA4F2F.384BF15@dio.com> <SJty5.3774$l35.74305@iad-read.news.verio.net> <39CB9A42.9BAAAE64@dio.com> <%%My5.3897$l35.81290@iad-read.news.verio.net>`

```
You are confusing a definition of what something IS with a statement of what that
something contains within it. In this case, you are asking for a definition of
socialism when what is really being discussed is a feature of what socialists
have asked for in their manifestoes and statements of principle. That is like
asking for a definition of Christianity when told that they pray to God.
Socialists routinely have decried and sought to eliminate profit, as they see
profit as the main instrument by which capitalists extract "surplus value" from
workers. Socialists want non-profit firms, but capitalists believe in using the
profit motive to organize markets. You can't be a capitalist and believe that
firms should make no profit, and you can't be much of a socialist if you believe
that firms ought to make a profit. One's attitude toward profit will depend on
ones alliance or disalliance with socialism, but is not a part of the definition
of socialism.

NA wrote:

> In article <39CB9A42.9BAAAE64@dio.com>, Dio  <Dio@dio.com> wrote:
> >
…[33 more quoted lines elided]…
> DD
```

```
Sorry, Laddie, you're the who's confused. Socialists don't believe
privately owned firms should be non-profit, they believe privately owned
firms should be non-existent. They believe the *state* should own the
means of production. THAT is a (IMHO, the) key tenet of socialism, not
one item on a laundry list of several dozen things they find attractive.

But, looking at the larger picture, whatever does this have to do with
COBOL? Are you a programmer? Have anything to do with the IT industry?
```

- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <39CEEB9D.486F2672@swedesoft.sw> <ibFz5.4289$l35.101574@iad-read.news.verio.net> <39CF9252.F99D3EB6@swedesoft.sw> <OuMz5.4348$l35.103809@iad-read.news.verio.net>`

```


NA wrote:

> In article <39CF9252.F99D3EB6@swedesoft.sw>,
> Sven Hawkins  <shawkins@swedesoft.sw> wrote:
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> daunted her so.

   Here is a goodplace for you to start: http://www.wsu.edu:8080/~brians/hum_303/manifesto.html It
is a study guide for the Communist Manifesto, which is also contained here:
http://www.anu.edu.au/polsci/marx/classics/manifesto.html

   Since you seem inexplicably unable to do your own research, here is a primer:

   Look up profit in the Online Encyclopedia of Marxism at
http://csf.colorado.edu/mirrors/marxists.org/glossary/index.htm and you will find: "See Surplus
Value". Look up surplus value and you will find:

------
Surplus Value

The basis of capitalist exploitation. Surplus value is the difference between the amount of
capital necessary to produce something, and the amount of capital the finished product is worth.
For example, a carpenter costs a capitalist $16 an hour, including supplies and total work hours
the total capital cost is $660. The only way moneybags can "make" money is by selling the product
the carpenter produced for a greater amount of money than it cost to produce. This difference in
cost is the surplus value.

Surplus value is created from labour power, which is unique among all other commodities in that it
has the ability to create value. Labourers (in order to keep their job!) must create more value
than what they are paid (in the most basic form of a wage ï¿½ the minimum wage ï¿½ established as the
minimum amount of money a labourer needs to survive). This extra value labourers create, the
surplus value, is owned by the capitalist who buys the labourer's time, and it is the sole source
of wealth for the capitalists. Marx explained that the amount of surplus value extracted from a
labourer is "an exact expression for the degree of exploitation"

Increasing Surplus Value: The capitalist increases surplus value in two ways:

Absolute surplus value:
By increasing the length of the working day (eg. overtime) and/or by making work more intensive
(speeding-up the production-line).

Relative surplus value:
By lowering wages

Through the ownership of surplus value the capitalist always overpowers the labourer ï¿½ it is with
this surplus value that the capitalist makes their own decisions on how to manage and use the
productive forces that they own, leaving the labourer still to work, while the capitalist rules
the world.

------

   Thus we see here that Marxism believes profit is the means by which workers are exploited by
capitalists. Obviously the goal of Marxism is the elimination of exploitation by capitalists,
which we see here therefore means the elimination of profit. If you are still not convinced, check
out http://csf.colorado.edu/mirrors/marxists.org/archive/marx/works/index.htm where you will find
a complete online index and text for Marx and Engels' works. You ought to also read the surplus
value sections III, IV and V of the work Das Kapital at
http://csf.colorado.edu/mirrors/marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1867-c1/index.htm . Also check out
the Principles of Communism
http://csf.colorado.edu/mirrors/marxists.org/archive/marx/works/1840/prin-com.htm where Marx goes
beyond merely eliminating profit but also inheritance:

------
The main measures, emerging as the necessary result of existing relations, are the following:

(i) Limitation of private property through progressive taxation, heavy inheritance taxes,
abolition of inheritance through collateral lines (brothers, nephews, etc.) forced loans, etc.

(ii) Gradual expropriation of landowners, industrialists, railroad magnates and shipowners, partly
through competition by state industry, partly directly through compensation in the form of bonds.
------

   Well, that ought to be enough to get you going. It is obvious from reading Marx that the
elimination of profit was a major plank by which it was thought that the exploitation of the
proletariat would end.
```

```
from a laborer "an exact expression for the degree of exploitation"'
(Marx's words in double-quotes).

If this surplus value were *not* 'extracted' - if it were allowed to
remain with the laborer, or the collective - then the amount extracted
would be zero and the degree of exploitation would be zero.

If the degree of exploitation is zero, then, socialism would have
eliminated oppression... and retained the profit.
```

```
state.  Now the state is not usually such a good manger, so the profit is
usually less.  But the elimination of profit is not a goal and is not
required under socialism.
```

```
Dio <Dio@dio.com> wrote:

> Demand for food in general
>is inelastic, but demand for a specific type of food item is not, and
…[3 more quoted lines elided]…
>for all workers by raising the wages of all workers.

sometimes this is true, and sometimes it isn't. yes, if you raise
labor rates, the product can go up in price, or it can go down. if i
pay my worker more, and they somehow manage to use that money wisely
(say they get a phone at home, instead of the mail) their time that
can be devoted to work goes *up*. alittle discretionary money for
workers can do wonders to an economy. or.... if the worker is always
trying to make ends meet, and the boss has to fire and hire
individuals all the time because they can't meet rent or other
payments, the price also goes up.

on the other hand, if management *or* the workers get paid extravagant
amounts for doing nothing, it also causes the economy to lose value,
because the hard working person who is at the bottom will resent the
person at the top who is wasting their time and yet never loses their
position.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ NAFTA

_(reply depth: 10)_

- **From:** "keith smyth" <k17731NO@SPAMMhome.com>
- **Date:** 2000-09-24T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,alt.computer.consultants,comp.lang.cobol,us.issues.occupations.computer-programmers
- **Message-ID:** `<CPsz5.359$583.32719@news1.onlynews.com>`
- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <8q5rf7$7sq$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <0Awx5.794$D83.53886@nnrp3.sbc.net> <39C6A322.DFAF296@blarney.att.net> <39C75CF5.BD7D151E@bellatlantic.net> <39C76BAF.E831ECD4@brazee.net> <39C7BA1C.346875E@jay.jay.org> <39C7CBF8.48CDD91B@brazee.net> <8q9v9j$prq$1@slb3.atl.mindspring.net> <39C8FA90.EC2027BA@dio.com>`

```
(My apologies that this didn't get cross-posted correctly the first time.
It was intended to clear up misconceptions in the Washington Pravda ... H1B
discussion.  Since it went beyond H1Bs I wanted to start a new thread.)

NAFTA seems to be mentioned a lot in here.  There seem to be many gross
misconceptions about NAFTA in the USA.  I'm up in Canada where NAFTA is a
big issue.  It covers a very large part of our total economy and it is
always in the news.

First I will clear up the two misconceptions on NAFTA most relevant to this
newsgroup, which is supposed to be on computer related job issues.

-There are only 3 countries in NAFTA.  Initially NAFTA was between Canada
(30M people) and the USA (300M people).  A couple of years later Mexico was
added because your administration hoped it would boost the Mexican economy
thus reducing illegal immigration to the USA.

So just Canada, Mexico and the USA are in NAFTA, no Caribbean countries.

So programming jobs leaving the USA for anywhere other than Canada or Mexico
are doing so inspite of  NAFTA, not because of it.

-NAFTA has greatly benefitted the programming profession in the USA.

Whatever the effects on other industries, head offices in Canada have been
closed, along with the related IT shops.  The consulting firms that depended
on these head offices are suffering, and many of them have closed too.

Those jobs have moved to the USA.  Since we are in NAFTA we can get TN (?)
visas, rather than H1Bs and follow the jobs, if we have 4 year comp sci
college degrees (and since this is Canada, it is easy for the INS to verify
that we really have them).

Speaking more broadly for a moment, I'd like to add that people and
businesses in most sectors of the Canadian economy feel that their sectors
have lost out in NAFTA.  The gap between incomes in the USA and Canada has
been increasing at an alarming rate.  You folks now earn, on average, 35%
more than us.  The costs-of-livings are different, but they vary so much
across both our countries that they are hard to  compare.

I'd like to see NAFTA enlarged so it is more balanced, rather than havning
one country with 2/3 of the population of the organization, and two junior
partners.

The one thing I most like to see changed about NAFTA are implementing some
of requirements for entry they have for the Eurpoean Community:
1.  The government must already be free and democratically elected.
2.  Minimum labour standards.
3.  Minimum enviromental standards.
4.  Minimum vacation standards. (Most of the developed world gets 6 to 8
weeks.  In Canada we get 3.  With 2, you guys are oppressed.)

NAFTA can give Canada and the US enhanced influence over other countries
wanting to enter.  We should be using this influence to bring their
standards of living up to ours, bringing, for example, IT wages up.

May god help us if we waste our influence enhancing return-on-equity.
Shareholders may reap large profits in the short term, but I'd rather be a
poor person in a democracy, than a rich person in a dictratorship.

Keith
```

```


Margaret Bartley wrote:
...

> US economy and the Mexican economy is that in the US we have
> minimum wage laws that result in working people having money
> to spend. ....

Nonsense.  The difference between the U.S. and Mexican economy is (1)
they tolerate a much higher degree of institutionalized corruption than
we do and (2)they really are socialist.

Up until the (appx) 1910 revolution Mexico was doing quite well and was
a favored place for European immigration.  After the revolution, the
movers and shakers pulled up their capital and headed for other places. 
Many have done quite well in U.S. business.

The only rational behind the minimum wage laws are political.  They kill
opportunity and force people in to sanctioned pursuits.
```

```
In article <39C984E4.C618BFF8@dio.com>,
  Dio <Dio@dio.com> wrote:
>
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Corporations and given massive tax deductions for closing down plants.
But that's not the worst part of the NAFTA fiasco. The companies are
given massive tax breaks when they move operations in the new foreign
location.

http://www.bvu.edu/~mcfadden/1997web/FreshStart/business_reports/paying/
free.htm
What were these incentives for offshore production that Westinghouse
and the Commerce Department discussed with an eye toward improving?
Westinghouse runs its Caribbean Basin production out of Puerto Rico,
where under the Section 936 tax loophole, the company pays no U.S.
corporate taxes on its profits.  Gratis from the U.S. taxpayer,
Westinghouse received a $20 million tax break in 1992.  It gets this
every year.  However, a problem developed when wages in Puerto Rico
rose to around $4.25 per hour in the mid-1980s.  Another incentive had
to be invented for 'free trade' to keep working.  So the twin plant
concept was developed.  It works like this.  Westinghouse keeps its tax
exempt status in Puerto Rico, but it can now source out the labor
intensive aspects of its assembly work to low wage Caribbean Basin
countries.  By blending the wages in Puerto Rico with the 52 cent per
hour wages the company pays in the Dominican Republic, Westinghouse
arrived at a $2.25 an hour "blended wage."  Westinghouse management has
pointed out that this is one-fifteenth of the wages it had to pay on
the U.S. mainland.


http://www.essential.org/monitor/hyper/mm0893.html
Losing Jobs to 936
AMID THE POSTURING and pontificating in the U.S. Congress this summer
over the budget reconciliation process, lawmakers passed restrictions
on what Senator David Pryor, D- Arkansas, calls "the Mother of All Tax
Shelters:" Section 936 of the Internal Revenue Code. But the changes
are too little, too late for the 483 workers laid off in May 1993 when
Acme Boot closed its Clarksville, Tennessee plant. These workers are
only a few of the nearly 24,000 in the mainland United States who have
lost their manufacturing jobs due to Section 936, according to a recent
study by the Midwest Center for Labor Research (MCLR).

Also known as the Possessions Tax Credit, Section 936 exempts U.S.
corporations from paying federal income tax on profits generated by a
qualified Puerto Rican subsidiary at the cost of $3 billion a year to
the U.S. Treasury and at the expense of high-wage jobs in the mainland
United States. Yet the extent to which Section 936 benefits the Puerto
Rican economy is questionable. Companies accounting for 12.6 percent of
Section 936- related employment received 63.5 percent of the loophole�s
benefits, according to the April 1993 testimony of Samuel Sessions,
Deputy Assistant Secretary of the U.S. Treasury. The pharmaceutical
industry alone - including Pfizer , Merck and American Home Products -
reaped a tax benefit of $66,281 per Puerto Rican employee in 1989,
according to the U.S. Treasury Department. With such a tax break, says
Richard Leonard, director of special projects for the Oil, Chemical and
Atomic Workers Union (OCAW), "mainland U.S. workers could work 80 hours
a week for nothing and would not come close" to competing on wages with
jobs in Puerto Rico.
```

- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <8q5rf7$7sq$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <0Awx5.794$D83.53886@nnrp3.sbc.net> <39C6A322.DFAF296@blarney.att.net> <39C75CF5.BD7D151E@bellatlantic.net> <39C76BAF.E831ECD4@brazee.net> <39C7BA1C.346875E@jay.jay.org>`

```
On Sun, 24 Sep 3900 19:43:30, "keith smyth" <k17731NO@SPAMMhome.com> 
wrote:

> 4.  Minimum vacation standards. (Most of the developed world gets 6 to 8
> weeks.  In Canada we get 3.  With 2, you guys are oppressed.)
> 

Interesting article, Keith.  Thanks that was very informative.  

I gotta make one point though.  The U.S. is very fluid and things
are not always what they appear to be.  

The last time I held a "real job", back in 1994 or so, my employer 
offered flex hours as well as consolidated leave.  I received 9 days 
of paid holiday, 15 days sick leave and 20 days of vacation.  That's 4
weeks, not as good as some countries and better that the average you 
quote.

We started new employees with 3 weeks vacation, I had been there a 
while so I got 4 weeks.  We started employees with 3 weeks rather than
2 because the CEO wanted to attract experienced, seasoned workers.

The leave was consolidated into a total of 44 days off, we could take 
any leave we wanted to on any day.   We could roll over the days, cash
in days.  Anything we wanted.  It was enough leave that you could take
off a week off every other month, and still accue leave.  If you 
butted the leave together, you could take a two week vacation every 
quarter.  Of course, you'd be working on New Years day, Thanksgiving, 
or that old tradition, Columbus day, but who cares.  

44 days is almost 9 five day weeks off.  That's a two week vacation 
every quarter, plus another 4 days off.  

By working the Flex system, doing 8 ten hour days, you could work a 4 
day week every other week.  Some people did that.   When you're a 
programmer, what's the big whoop about working a ten hour day.  

I took a ton of time off and cashed in leave on a regular basis.   No 
problem, fill out a form, trot down to personnel and get a check for a
couple grand. 

Finally, time off and salary are interconvertable.  You'd (as a 
nation) earn 
more if you took less leave.  

Now that I'm a consultant, I don't get any time off.  On the other 
hand, I get to pick my assignments and give priority to clients who 
send me to Hawaii for a week at a time, this doesn't happen often 
enough but it has been known to occur. 
```

```
(My apologies that this didn't get crossposted correctly.)

It is not uncommon for very wealthy people to spend 2 hours each day
following politics, and to give hundreds of thousands of dollars in
donations of time and money to politcians.  They do not do this as a give
away.

The wealthy recognize that political involvement pays the best return on
equity they can get.

Read the presentation at the other end of this link (I didn't write it, but
sorry it is so lengthy.  It is worth finishing.  You might want to print it
off.) and tell me programmers and analysts don't need an organization to
represent us.

The case studies are incredible.

http://heather.cs.ucdavis.edu/itaa.real.html#tth_sEc5

If people are loosing their jobs because of age anyway, what do they have to
loose by trying to unionize their workplace?  Are we that much more
professional than airline pilots?  Programmers are unionized in countries
like Germany, including a lot of those employed by US firms.  It didn't hurt
the success of Software AG (makers of SAP).

Even young people would support unionization, once they are hired on,
because it makes no sense to study for 4 years to get a career that will
last only 8 years.

Even without unionization, as a group we are so disinterested in national
and international affairs.  We need to employ the tactics of  the NOW, the
Sierra Club, the NRA, and our employers.

We need to recognize that industry groups are employer groups, and they are
representing interests contrary to our own.

Write all congressmen, not just your own.  Write every month or two.  Be
polite, but be pointed.

- Keith
```

```
("lose," not "loose")

In a free society, folks may associate as they wish. Some associations 
are clubs, some are parties, some are "unions."

And in a free society, employment is by mutual consent. Which means an 
employer may choose to fire all those in a "union." Or he may choose to 
not allow union organizing on his time or in his facilitites or even in 
his parking lot.

And he may even send his own agents to inflitrate the union and to 
report back to him so he can take steps to get rid of the organizers and 
troublemakers.

All of these things are no business of government.

--Tim May
```

- **Message-ID:** `<39D0A7A2.6BF82759@brazee.net>`
- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <8q5rf7$7sq$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <0Awx5.794$D83.53886@nnrp3.sbc.net> <39C6A322.DFAF296@blarney.att.net> <39C75CF5.BD7D151E@bellatlantic.net> <39C76BAF.E831ECD4@brazee.net> <39C84CD0.59970393@dio.com> <39C5ECB6.2BCDDEC9@kg.hsanet.net> <39CAFB14.E7DD4E23@optonline.net> <8qg2qd$965$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <BPsz5.358$583.32719@news1.onlynews.com> <tcmay-46B538.14392824092000@news.got.net> <ojmvssg5adp967gdg0u30qak0l0n8pg0tk@4ax.com>`

```
G Moore wrote:

> on the other hand, having union officials for service industries,
> where the people really are expendable, is a joke, and that's really
> where they need them most. i'd rather see unions for fast food,
> agricultural, janitorial workers than programmers.

There are two reasons why fast food isn't unionized:
1.  Most employees are teens who don't expect to make it a career.
2.  It is a very competitive (small) business.  It is easy to start a
hamburger joint, and easy to close one.  Only a few workers are at any one
site, and there is another fast food joint down the street willing to hire
workers or take customers.

One opposite of this is the airline industry, where everything is very big,
expensive, and dependent upon skilled labor.  It is relatively easy for
labor to create tremendous costs.  It still has competition to grab market
share.

Another opposite is government, with its monopoly.
```

```
because it is free, i'll get no technical support. of course, if i
were to buy a new computer today, it might be a cybiko or webtv. but i
have old software, and i figure (hell this is a cobol newsgroup) the
reason alot of things don't change is because of old software....

the other thing i am worried is that i am a technical worker. used to
program, couldn't handle keeping up to date on all the customers
specs. but recently my co has been buying these new machines with the
cdrom, network card, modem, and vga card pre-installed. so it's about
20 minutes of my work per computer i no longer get paid for. well,
anyways, i figure i'm gonna have to start learning again, possibly
learning how to drill holes in walls and run cables (eventually we
might buy wireless network cards, but right now, the tech isn't there)
for nic's or adsl modems. and still, the consumer market doesn't buy
nics. well, anyways, the moral of the story is that shipping jobs
overseas doesn't make a new work appear here.

perhaps a better story is a gas station. you can only drive one car at
a time (if the roads allow it), which means a gas station owner can
only sell so much gasoline.

Howard Brazee <howard@brazee.net> wrote:

>> jobs of their non-immigrant superiors. this is the true nature of h1b,
>> that they don't win today, but they do tommorrow.

>If they are inferior, how do they take the jobs?  Or by "superior", do you mean
>"people who look like you"?

by superior, i mean people who have used the technology. let me try an
example. let's pretend you have joe who writes cobol code for the
bank. then mary comes in from india, displaces the current janitor,
learns the ropes, and displaces joe. i don't believe in anyway there
is a mary who leaves their more industrial country to come to a dinky
country with their superior skills. there's no motive. but i do
believe mary who leaves a dinky village will move to a more industrial
society, displace a non-industrial job, and learn industrial skills
can then displace an industrial worker. that's the true nature of h1b.
it doesn't displace joe today, but it does tommorrow. 

i am not really against h1b. i am against having an immigrant come
here with their own income, and the sponsoring company not cover their
wayback fee. ok, i guess my idea is kinda stupid. the richer companies
which already do cut costs will have no trouble fronting the
immigrants ticket back, but the poorer businesses won't. and i know
the immigrant probably will fight like a dog to stay here. so i guess
the real problem is ensuring the people who get displaced *can* find a
new job without requiring them to front the education cost. 12 years
behind a desk without any choice of marketable skills is too much.

reply to email gvwmoore@spam.ix.netcom.com remove the spam
```

```
"Jacques Bernier" <jbernier@microtec.net> wrote:
>
> Except when "essential services" are threatened. Then big gov.
> legislate them back to work.
>
> But that's ok, right?

Yep, it is not only right, it is necessary.

The government represents *all* of its citizens.  A strike held by
government employees turns the government function into a tool for a
small segment of citizens.  They are using their special position to
further their own aims, against the public interest.  Just as nepotism
and other uses of government position for personal gain is criminal,
so is a strike by government employees, for exactly the same reasons.
The entire concept violates what government stands for.  After all,
no one, except military draftees, is forced into government work.
Military personnel, to say the least, cannot strike, nor should they
be permitted to.  If you don't like the heat, stay out of the kitchen.
It's very simple: if you want to strike, don't work for the government.

Freedom isn't free, guy.  There are costs associated with maintaining
freedom.  "Freedom" for anybody to do anything they like at any time
isn't freedom, it's anarchy.
```

```
"philotsopher" <philotsopher@my-deja.com> wrote:
>
> So what do you expect their union to do, work for 3rd world salaries?
> There will never be a compromise good enough if you are competing
> against cheap labor. I think you are getting "efficient foreign steel"
> factories with 3rd world sweatshops with no environmental restraints.

What I *expect* them to do is sit idle, earning nothing, instead of
working and making somewhat less than before.  What would have made
far more sense, would have been to accept some temporary cuts so
they could earn a living, making their employers more profitable,
while the steel companies did their part to enhance efficiency and
competitiveness.  If I find that I can't get work for what I now
charge, you can be sure that I will be willing to work for less,
rather than starve.  I am thankful that I am able to make a very
good living doing pleasant work.  But I don't believe the world owes
me that, just because I like it or want it or already have it.

Were you paying attention?  The unions voted to take *no* cuts of
any kind.  Zip.  Nada.  They didn't just reject severe cuts, they
rejected *any* cuts, of any kind, when 75% of them were already laid
off, their plants shut down.  Smart.

A while back, every major publication in the world was type set using
linotype machines.  A linotype operator typed in the text, and the
machine cast the letters from hot lead.  The operators of these
machines were highly unionized.  In the 1970's there was some effort
to start computerizing the process, for better efficiency and quality.
The strong linotype unions shut off that effort completely.  That is,
until the technology became good enough that the industry computerized
virtually overnight, completely eliminating all the linotype jobs in a
very short time.  In fact, Rupert Murdock (admittedly not my favorite
person) actually did without warning fire all his linotype operators
operators at the close of one work day.  Why?  Because they striked
and shut down his publications at the suggestion of computerization.
If he had given them any warning, they would have put him out of
business.  Instead of accepting the slow change, and gradually
eliminating the profession over many years, probably through attrition,
the unions held back the flood until the dam literally burst.  Once
again, smart, very smart.

One of my cousins was a construction worker on a building in Detroit
a number of years ago.  There were three main unions working on the
job, and each union had a different lunch hour.  The problem was, all
of the union workers had to be working for the others to do their
jobs.  So the construction company was paying for two extra hours
every day, while the workers sat idle, waiting for the other union
workers.  What do you think that did to the company's profit line and
competitiveness?  Do you think the unions cared?  Sure, they really
help the union employees, by running the companies they work for into
the ground, through such moronic practices.
```

- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <8q5rf7$7sq$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <0Awx5.794$D83.53886@nnrp3.sbc.net> <39C6A322.DFAF296@blarney.att.net> <39C75CF5.BD7D151E@bellatlantic.net> <39C76BAF.E831ECD4@brazee.net> <39C84CD0.59970393@dio.com> <39C5ECB6.2BCDDEC9@kg.hsanet.net> <39CAFB14.E7DD4E23@optonline.net> <8qg2qd$965$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <BPsz5.358$583.32719@news1.onlynews.com> <39CE892A.5EE9DFC5@juno.com> <39cea52c.4820961@news.globetrotter.net> <0cJz5.2345$103.16203@news1.atl> <8qo24n$vj7$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <eRMz5.2758$103.18067@news1.atl> <39D01744.93177590@net.gr>`

```
>    Typical union. Union members tend not to be the brightest bulbs in the
> pack, which is why they argue for unions in the first place. Self-aware
> mediocrity aggregates itself together when left to its own devices.

I was going to say that an incorrect stereotype, but I've actually never
heard it said before.

Someone doing stoop labor is much less likely to be unionized than a
tradesperson.  In the US, airline pilots are unionized.  In Canada,
physicians, university professors, crown attorneys (like DAs) are also
unionized.  In Germany, almost everyone is unionized, including programmers
and engineers.  (Note that some of these unions are called associations, but
nonetheless they engage in collective bargaining.)

Ordinarily I am not a union supporter.  But there are pendulms and pendulums
swing, and when companies treat their IT workers like dirt, that is a
situation that cannot be allowed to stand.  Sure unions sometimes become too
strong.  (But not so far as their members are concerned.  Ask the airline
pilots.)  But these are usually unions associated with monopoly, oligopoly,
or government organizations.  M$ workers would be the only ones having to
worry about too much power.  And employees throw their weight around at
their own peril.  There are examples of unions wrecking their industry, not
so much in the US, legislators and the courts favor companies  But British
dockworkers and coal miners destroyed their own jobs by getting carried
away with their power.

I do not see any other way programmers, analysts and project leaders are
going to correct problems in the relations between the profession and
employers.  Do you folks think the Programmers Guild is going anywhere?

I fear the computer industry will die in the US, and the problem will not be
unionization, but the lack of it.

Only truely stupid kids go to school for 4 years to get a degree that will
only be good for 8 years.

Only dweebs sit back and let their employers discriminate against them on
the basis of age or anything else, without at least attempting to stand up
for their rights.  And let's be honest.  There are lots of dweebs in IT.

I was around and I was old enough to follow politics back when the textile
and steel industries left the USA.  There seem to be some misconceptions
that have occurred about why this happened, perhaps spread by management.

When the textile industry left the USA it mostly went to the former West
Germany (textiles not garments).  Salaries were higher there than in US
states the industry was leaving.  The workers were much more likely to be
unionized there than in the US.  And labor laws were stiffer.

Salaries were not important because production was so automated.  What
counted was efficient well trained skilled workers who could run and
maintain the machines.  Germany had them.  Alabama, etc. didn't.   (Since
then, the textile industry may have moved again.  It was back in the 70s and
80s that it left the US.)

The steel industry left for Japan and Korea.  Japan paid well, Korea just
okay.  Again, it was not salaries.  The plants in Japan and Korea were more
modern, the workers better trained, and the foreign operations had higher
productivity even though they had to import both the iron ore and tremendous
amounts of coal.

All these well trained H1Bs going back to India are going to result in India
having a better trained and more experienced IT workforce than the US.  IF
we are going to let them in, we need to keep them here.

But besides that, programmers in many regions and at many companies are
finding very poor working conditions.  You might have it good at the moment.
But what are you going to do if your employer folds, or gets bought out.

Companies are trusting HR departments and managers without professional
backgrounds to do proper recruiting, and they are recruiting on the basis of
age and the version of products you know.

Twenty years after receiving their degrees, most comp sci graduates are no
longer in their field.  This is a much lower retention than other
professions.  Check out the following link for some disturbing
documentation:

http://heather.cs.ucdavis.edu/pub/Immigration/ImmigAndComputerIndustry/SVRep
ort.html

"Self aware mediocrity" makes manager in 2 years.  That is reality today.

- Keith
```

```
Mark, what city are you in, what part of the industry, and what level?

I do genuinely wonder how large the problem is.  Is it localized to the high
tech part of IT?  Is it localized to certain regions?

There are fat people living in Ethiopia where others are dying from hunger.
The fact that some are well fed in Ethiopia doesn't mean the others are
faking the death by hunger.  At the same time, there are anorexics dying of
malnutrition in the USA.  That some are dying from lack of food in the here
doesn't mean we have a food shortage.

It's really nice that you and your buddies have it good.  That you are fat
and happy, so to speak, proves nothing.  That you got your last job in a
week proves nothing.  That the problem is not completely pervasive doesn't
mean it doesn't exist.

From the attached link, from postings in Monster.com, and from 20 years of
varied experience in IT, I have no doubt that there is a real problem.  I do
wonder the about the size of it.

> > When the textile industry left the USA it mostly went to the former West
> > Germany (textiles not garments).  Salaries were higher there than in US
> > states the industry was leaving.  The workers were much more likely to
be
> > unionized there than in the US.  And labor laws were stiffer.
>
>    LOL. Yeah, how many clothes say "Made in Germany"? All mine that aren't
made
> here

The textile industry makes the cloth.  The garment industry makes the
clothes.  (That was what my parenthetic comment meant.)

The textile industry is and was at the time very highly automated.  The
garment manufacturing industry has a high manual component.  The textile
industry in fact moved to Germany.  Wages were not the reason it moved.
That Germans made better cloth for less using their superior skills was.

Tom Peters, the management guru does a seminar in which he details this.

> > The steel industry left for Japan and Korea.  Japan paid well, Korea
just
> > okay.  Again, it was not salaries.  The plants in Japan and Korea were
more
> > modern, the workers better trained, and the foreign operations had
higher
> > productivity even though they had to import both the iron ore and
tremendous
> > amounts of coal.
>
>    What does this have to do with unions?
>

The claim had been made in posts that the steel industry left the USA due to
unionization.  This was not the case.  It left because these other countries
had the expertise and tools to make better steel for less.

My concern is that the current trend of not training computer people here is
going to again put us up against other countries that have better trained
workforces.

> > Twenty years after receiving their degrees, most comp sci graduates are
no
> > longer in their field.
>
>    Management is not in the compsci field. If they were still programming
> instead of managing after twenty years that wolud be a bad sign.
>

Fewer than 13% entered management.  The others left for other reasons.

> >  This is a much lower retention than other
> > professions.
>
>    Because programmers usually have the smarts to move up in their
organizations
> faster.

Actually, 18% of civil engineers, the "Arts of Engineering" moved into
management in the same time interval.  Just follow the link below for the
source.

I'm not offering an opinion on H1Bs.  This thread is on the problem of IT
people being mistreated by employers and the government as documented in:

http://heather.cs.ucdavis.edu/pub/Immigration/ImmigAndComputerIndustry/SVRep
ort.html

and what we should do about it.

- Keith
```

```
So you are saying that 420,000 H1Bs are issued per year, they are good for
one year, and only 40,000 left this year.  And that once someone goes past
the time period for the H1B they never leave.  That would make your 10%
calculation accurate.

But is not 420,000 the total number of H1Bs outstanding?  And are they not
valid for up to 6 years.  And do not some people leave after getting other
visas or green cards, or even citizenship?  And does not the number of H1Bs
issued from year to year vary?

I'm sure I've seen numbers of close to 2/3 returning home, but if you
have a link to a reliable site ...

Mark, a tip.  No offense, but your name calling and accusations merely make
your arguments look less credible and more suspect, regardless of their
actual merit.

I was not in on that earlier discussion, or if I was, not on any part where
there was definitive evidence given of anything other than way more than 10%
of H1Bs returning home.  Remember, most of these postings are cross posted,
but perhaps not all.  And what we each see is only what our separate
newsgroup server provides us.

So just make your arguments and refrain from the other stuff.  And if you
have a link documenting the 10% claim, please post it.

Thanks.

- Keith
```

```
On Mon, 25 Sep 2000 18:30:58, "Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net> 
wrote:

> A while back, every major publication in the world was type set using
> linotype machines.  A linotype operator typed in the text, and the
> machine cast the letters from hot lead.  The operators of these
> machines were highly unionized.  In the 1970's there was some effort

The power didn't flow from the union, the ETAOIN machine was hard to 
use.  In the 1960's, I saw one demonstrated at a vocational school that
a pal attended.  

> to start computerizing the process, for better efficiency and quality.
> The strong linotype unions shut off that effort completely.  That is,
> until the technology became good enough that the industry computerized
> virtually overnight, completely eliminating all the linotype jobs in a

Likely, it would have played out exactly the same, union or no union. 
The first computer systems were not that good, too many things had to 
come together to make it work. 

In the late 1960's We used to typeset manuals on tuned up IBM 1403
printers using carbon film ribbons.   This gave us one font per page
and no graphics.  

It wasn't until laser printers became available that you could replace 
the Linotype.

The 1970's opened with IBM S/370 155's which had 4 meg of memory, and 
3211 chain-driven impact printers.  It closed with the 4341 and laser 
printers, the Wang photocomposition system, etc. 

> very short time.  In fact, Rupert Murdock (admittedly not my favorite
> person) actually did without warning fire all his linotype operators
> operators at the close of one work day.  Why?  Because they striked
> and shut down his publications at the suggestion of computerization.
> If he had given them any warning, they would have put him out of

This doesn't make sense either.  If Rupie could switch overnight, why 
did he fire them?  He could have asked nicely for concessions and when
they balked, showed them the IBM 4506 terminal and said, "no, you don't
understand, your people *are* going to be reclassed as computer
composition terminal operators, we'll keep their salaries as-is for 3
years, straight time for overtime, not time and a half, they'll work in
comfortable, cool, quiet offices, a big change from the clank and 
clatter of the old hot-type technology,  likely we won't need new
employees but if we do hire, they will earn 70% of scale, we
will work this out."

Then, if they chose to strike, he still had his options open.

> business.  Instead of accepting the slow change, and gradually
> eliminating the profession over many years, probably through attrition,
> the unions held back the flood until the dam literally burst.  Once
> again, smart, very smart.

I don't think it was quite that black and white, nor do I think the 
unions were that, well, dumb.  The technology did change quickly.  
What would slow change be?  Connecting a cardpunch to a hot-type 
machine one year.  Then replacing the keypunch with a 2741 the next?  
Putting a ETAOIN keyboard on a 3277, replacing the 3277 (a fixed font 
CRT terminal) with a Xerox STAR?  

Surprisingly, the old Linotype profession has a new incarnation.  We 
call our modern "fonter's", webmasters. 
```

```
Cory, what you say merely serves to support what I said.  The point
is that computer typesetting in the 1970's *was* very poor, and not
a real threat to the linotype operators.  It would have taken some
20 years to completely replace them.  But instead of letting the
natural process work, the unions shut down any publication that
tried to computerize to any degree.  I well remember the newspaper
articles of the time, but my information comes from a number of
newspaper and magazine articles, and a TV documentary about Murdock.
The part about Rupert Murdock is completely true.  It cost Murdock
a lot more money, and it was a big risk, to switch over to the
computers all at once.  Even Murdock, slime as he is, is not a fool
when it comes to his pocketbook.  He had to build the computer press
center in complete secrecy.  The unions would have only gone on
strike if he did not have the center ready, and if he did, why keep
paying union people who were doing him no favors?  True, Murdock is
a low life, but I really believe he was forced into this by the
boneheaded unions.
```

```
ProNews/2 User <kiyoinc@ATTGlobal.XOUT.net> -

I use the same anology of linotype opearators.  The machines requiring quite
a bit of technical skill to operate, and technolgy moving on making them
totally obsolete.

I saw this in my previous career as a building inspector.  The linotype
became obsolete in the matter of a few years.

It became obsolete in small un-unionized shops too.  It was a matter of a
couple of years and all linotype operators faced unemployment.

The linotype operators actually got retraining or early retirement if they
worked at large unionized newspapers.  I did consulting at one major paper
where the LAN admins and font designers used to be linotype operators.

Of course some newspaper unions were so powerful they drove their employers
out of business.   That is not a good result.  That is hopefully not the
result we would have with trained professionals.

The other analogy I frequently use is scribners.  These are the people who
used to make copies before there were automated means to do so.  They copied
on document by hand to make another.

They too had a skilled profession.  But it passed.

- Keith
```

```
"Phil Eamon" <phileam@blarney.att.net> wrote in message
news:39D4045A.A1266C54@blarney.att.net...
> keith smyth wrote:
> >
> > ProNews/2 User <kiyoinc@ATTGlobal.XOUT.net> -
> >
> > I use the same anology of linotype opearators.  The machines requiring
quite
> > a bit of technical skill to operate, and technolgy moving on making them
> > totally obsolete.
<<snip>>
> >
> > They too had a skilled profession.  But it passed.
> Your parallels are wrong for the context. There aren't any obsolescent
> professions in sight, just the opposite--the profession is doing very
> well, thank you.

Printing is still composed, but the skills required have been downgraded,
and the number of people required to do it has dropped dramatically.

For years the software industry has worked on tools that will dramatically
increase programmer productivity, in order to reduce the number of
programmers necessary to complete a project.

For years the software industry has worked on reducing the skill and
intelligence required to program computers.

For years the software industry has worked on creating packages to replace
custom written systems, in order to reduce the number of programmers
necessary to provide a function, or to eliminate them entirely.

For years the hardware and operating systems industry has worked on making
operating systems and hardware easy enough to install without the need for
specialist help.

And for years educators have worked on making elementary computer
programming a skill ever person graduates with.

These groups aren't there yet ... but they aren't done yet.

That aside, I think you would concede that one day Visual Basic will be
obsolete, and VB programmers will face the same difficulties that COBOL
programmers face today, even discounting age discrimination.

Some of us even imagine a world where the computers largely program
themselves.

- Keith


>What happens is that there's a concerted effort by some
> players in the market to bypass the market mechanism via non-market
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> > - Keith
```

- **Message-ID:** `<39D015F2.3E78DBB9@net.gr>`
- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <8q5rf7$7sq$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <0Awx5.794$D83.53886@nnrp3.sbc.net> <39C6A322.DFAF296@blarney.att.net> <39C75CF5.BD7D151E@bellatlantic.net> <39C76BAF.E831ECD4@brazee.net> <39C84CD0.59970393@dio.com> <39C5ECB6.2BCDDEC9@kg.hsanet.net> <39CAFB14.E7DD4E23@optonline.net> <8qg2qd$965$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <BPsz5.358$583.32719@news1.onlynews.com> <39CE892A.5EE9DFC5@juno.com> <39cea52c.4820961@news.globetrotter.net> <0cJz5.2345$103.16203@news1.atl> <8qo24n$vj7$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <39CF97CF.52C55AB7@brazee.net>`

```


Howard Brazee wrote:

> philotsopher wrote:
>
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> cheaply, or if someone else produces shoes cheaply, we still lose our jobs.

  No, this is a naive approach. If we buy steel from overseas then foreigners
have more dollars with which to buy American goods. It actually is even simpler
than that, because the only way the rest of the world will sell us steel is if
they know that they will get something back in return that is at least as
valuable to them as the resources they used up in the production of that steel,
whether that something takes the form of goods, assets or services. The
financial value of *everything* that leaves the US in trade is always exactly
equal to the financial value of *everything* that enters the US in trade (where
"everything" includes both goods, services and financial assets). Currently we
are selling the rest of the world financial instruments and assets in sufficient
amounts to offset the flow of financial instruments and assets into the country,
a surplus in financial and service goods that offsets the deficit in goods (the
"trade deficit" refers to goods and services but not financial instruments and
assets), which means that some of the money going overseas when we buy foreign
goods is coming back into the US in the form of investment, with the rest coming
back in the form of purchases of American exports. Those American exports create
jobs, as do the companies and industries that expand as they make use of the
investment funds that come in. It is a good question whether or not the jobs
created this way offset the number lost, and when the economy is growing as fast
as it is now and is at such low unemployment it is doubtful that any costs
caused by free trade are doing any harm at all right now. In fact, economic
theory is pretty solidly behind the idea that more value is created than lost by
free trade.

   It is misleading to look at only one side of international trade. It is a
bilateral process.
```

```
Petros Economidis wrote:

> Howard Brazee wrote:
>
…[36 more quoted lines elided]…
> bilateral process.

Not if you are a steel worker.  If you are a steel worker, you have three choices:

1.  Fight to keep your pay high, until you're unemployed.
2.  Fight to keep competitive, so that the steel industry keeps you employed.
3.  Develop skills which take advantage of the new economy where cheap foreign steel
helps create wealth here.

If you are a union boss in the steel industry, you will probably spend all your
effort on option 1.  That is unless you care about your members.
```

```
better to be looking for another job while earning income, than while
earning none?  Duh!
```

```
philotsopher wrote:

>
> You are so naive it's almost sickening.

Most people call me cynical.  These are opposites.

> Our politicians are totally
> captive to corporate money. They are basically administrators to our
> corporations. Power in our society is owned by those with the money.

Yes.  Do you prefer the power centralized or spread out?  Where money is a
large source of the power, and lots of people have money, nobody has all the
power.  Unions have some power, corporations have some power, the Sierra
club has some power, the NRA has some power.  In places where corporations
do not have such power, a Stalin has it all, and can kill millions of his
people with impunity.

There WILL be people with power.  That is a fact of life.  Power corrupts,
and those with power will abuse.  If the power is concentrated, we will be
in bigger danger than when it is spread out.  If one power fights another
power, it might not fight so hard against us.
```

- **From:** "keith smyth" <k17731NO@SPAMMhome.com>
- **Date:** 2000-09-27T05:21:17+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,alt.computer.consultants,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<htfA5.7813$583.306022@news1.onlynews.com>`
- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <8q5rf7$7sq$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <0Awx5.794$D83.53886@nnrp3.sbc.net> <39C6A322.DFAF296@blarney.att.net> <39C75CF5.BD7D151E@bellatlantic.net> <39C76BAF.E831ECD4@brazee.net> <39C84CD0.59970393@dio.com> <39C5ECB6.2BCDDEC9@kg.hsanet.net> <39CAFB14.E7DD4E23@optonline.net> <8qg2qd$965$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <BPsz5.358$583.32719@news1.onlynews.com> <39CF6EB7.FEF5A46@brazee.net> <8qo2eb$72$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <39CF9D88.4A1D81A7@brazee.net> <8qr6c6$hrh$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
Agreed phil.

Unions only have a place where there is an imbalance between the power of
the employees and the power of the employer, with the current situation
being excessive employer power.

An imbalance either way is not good for the countries economic health, or
for freedom and prosperity (the prosperity of all citizens).

Just because some unions in the past acted irresponsibly does justify the
prejudice that all unions do.

A lot of the workplace safety legislation and overtime laws we have are due
to the work of unions, and of other groups representing employees.  Someone
mentioned the Triangle Shirtwaist fire.  In that a hundred and some odd
women died because management chained and locked the fire exits.  And there
have been many mining disasters due to corners being cut in safety.  And in
construction (and King Crab fishing) this disregard for worker life
continues.

In the past there have been, and there continues today, to be employers who
expose their workers to physical hazards, hazardous chemicals, and a
standard of living only slightly above poverty.

Thankfully that is not the case for us (assuming that our VDTs really are
safe).

That said, efficiency for employees is how much money make and how many
perks we get for how much effort.

Like our managers, we should be trying to improve our efficiency.  Unlike
our managers we lack the inside knowledge to know our own value.

Like business owners and shareholders, we should be trying to improve the
return on our investment.  Unlike shareholders, we are an integral part of
our employing company.  We can't just hop onto Ameritrade and get a new
employer, not every month anyway.  That is why shareholders have done so
well on increasing their return on investment.

My feeling is we need something more in our profession.  Not at every
company.  Most companies are run fine.

We certainly need a  lobby group to put forward our concerns to legislators.
Our employers have done so.  The large consulting firms did so.  They have
lobbied to restrict our freedoms.  We should defend ourselves.

But at those companies where people are being treated poorly anyway, where
prospects are misrepresented, where people are expected to work unpaid
overtime, not trained and not upgraded, laid off because they loyaly did y2k
work instead of playing around on Java, and where age or other
discrimination is rampant, those companies deserve to be unionized.  A mere
lobby group would be like bringing a knife to a gun fight in those
situations.

- Keith

"philotsopher" <philotsopher@my-deja.com> wrote in message
news:8qr6c6$hrh$1@nnrp1.deja.com...
```

```
have

the millions of dollars spent by pro employer lobby groups? They seem
to think that this money is being spent to improve their working
conditions. What a bunch of sheep!

where
> prospects are misrepresented, where people are expected to work unpaid
> overtime, not trained and not upgraded, laid off because they loyaly
did y2k
> work instead of playing around on Java, and where age or other
> discrimination is rampant, those companies deserve to be unionized.
A mere
```

```


philotsopher wrote:

> In article <htfA5.7813$583.306022@news1.onlynews.com>,
>   "keith smyth" <k17731NO@SPAMMhome.com> wrote:
…[10 more quoted lines elided]…
> combat that.

   ROTFLMAO. You simply want to overlay one level of power-hungry people
with yet another layer of power-hungry weasels. The problem is not a lack of
power-hungry weasels. I've seen zero evidence that having a union does
anyone any good these days. (Look at United. They are OWNED by their
employees and they still are having labor problems.)

> > An imbalance either way is not good for the countries economic
> health, or
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> class.

   No, increased productivity through the application of modern technology
and better production methods is the reason why the US has such a large
middle class. Labor unions have traditionally acted to reduce productivity
and so are irrelevant to the economic well-being of our middle class.

>
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> They conveniently leave out what would happen without the unions.

   Given how small a percentage of the workers in America are now unionized
(17% or something like that), it seems pretty obvious that what would happen
without the unions IS what is happening right now. Unprecedented prosperity
and stability.

>
>
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> Unions would disappear.

   That's a stupid argument. Do you mean to say that all the gains you think
laborers have made that you attribute to past union activity would somehow
just disappear if the proportion of union workers fell from 17% to 0%? What
precisely do you think that 17% are doing that is so important to the
welfare of the other 83% and the maintenance of those gains you attribute to
them?

> They only know that the last 7 years have been
> good to them, and they figure the US has always been like that.

   Hell, no. I know it was much worse when unions were more powerful.

>
> Corporations love ignorant populations for exploitation.
>
   So do union propagandists like you.

> Isn't it weird how some of these anti union types see no threat from
> the millions of dollars spent by pro employer lobby groups?

   I hope that the firm I work for gets all sorts of govt breaks and
advantages at your expense, as it will increase the value of my stock
options. Aside from that, on a more serious note, it is important to realize
that most of that money is spent opposing itself, as one group of companies
lobby for what benefits them while their competitors lobby for what benefits
those competitors, thus cancelling each other out. This operates on a far
larger level than you might think too, as what benefits workers who make the
products of one group of firms thereby also benefits the consumers of the
products of another group of firms, and what reduces costs for one group of
firms thereby reduces the prices paid by the consumers of the products
produced by those firms. The idea that only the very very rich benefit from
the activities of firms is just plain stupid. When it comes to money spent
by the entire corporate world as a whole, aside from this internecine
conflict, even that comes around to the consumer. In a competitive
environment, costs get passed on to the consumer and whatever lowers those
costs thereby also lowers the costs that get passed on to the consumer.
   Labor unions stress the alleged benefit that they are seeking for people
in their tole as workers but those same actions work AGAINST those same
people in their role as consumers. Higher wages ARE higher costs and if
productivity has not changed then workers as a whole CAN NOT be any better
off simply because they get higher wages. The higher wages that the unions
got for them do no additional good for the workers because costs went up the
same amount. This game of musical chairs might impress the less intelligent
among our species but nobody who is intelligent is going to think that
merely raising all workers' wages will do ANY good whatsoever. Therefore, a
labor union can only succeed in improving the financial welfare of their
members if they manage to get larger wage increases than other workers in
the economy get. It is a beggar-thy-neighbor policy. In other words, it is
human greed at its worst.
   The only things that will improve worker welfare overall for the whole
economy is whatever will increase productivity or directly increase human
happiness. Unions may improve human happiness without offsetting costs if
they get employers to engage in activities that don't increase costs so much
that they offset the increase in human happiness that results (I think that
getting all firms to offer 4 weeks of vacation a year even to new employees
would be one such measure). This seems to rarely happen in the real world
though, as unions bargain for higher salaries more than anything else, or
they'll bargain for things that actually reduce overall productivity, like a
prohibition of the use of non-union labor.
   Truly stupid people often think that any concessions labor unions win are
a total gain for workers, never stopping to wonder exactly who PAYS for
those increased benefits. The stupidest ones of all think it will come out
of the executives' salaries. These are the ones that are convinced to
agitage for unions by quotations of huge executive salaries, as if the
executives will just be so impressed by union activities that they will open
up their wallets and share the contents instead of merely passing the costs
on to the consumers (who are, surprise, the middle class).

> They seem
> to think that this money is being spent to improve their working
> conditions. What a bunch of sheep!

   That perfectly describes unionheads who think their dues are going toward
improving their own welfare when actually they are just subsidizing
cost-push inflation and liberal pinhead politicians (though conservative
pinhead politicians would probably be no improvement).

> > But at those companies where people are being treated poorly anyway,
> where
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
> > situations.

   Those places often can't get unionized because they are usually bankrupt
in time, and bankruptcy is a far worse fate than unionization. Unionization
mostly victimizes consumers, unless the union gets really really stupid
(which seems quite frequent). It is such a rare thing for the very wealthy
to be hurt by union activity that it can be considered statistically
insignificant.
```

```
either. That paper is a good example of how NOT to do an economic analysis.

> loose by trying to unionize their workplace?

   Unions don't work when capital is mobile intellectual capital. The whole idea
of a union is to withhold (or more usually threaten to withhold) labor until the
owners of capital give the workers more stuff. The model is totally inapplicable
when the capital is intellectual capital that is contained within the workers
instead of being owned by the business owners.
```
