```
"Paul Raulerson" <praul@cegi.no-spam-net> wrote:
>
> Well, on the other hand, let me point out that unions formed in the
> 1800's in response to business practices that would make Malaysian
> sweat shops embarrassed.

True enough.  However, patients used to be blead to 'cure' disease.
Just because there was a reason for doing it, doesn't mean the
'solution' chosen was a good or apt one. :-)

Have you considered what would have happened if those millions of
workers had politically pressed for something like OSHA with their
voting ballots, instead of using coertion, through unions?  For one
thing, there would have been much less needless bloodshed.  Just
as the U.S. Civil War was stupid, because slavery could have been
abolished in 10 or 20 years with little or no bloodshed, by wise
use of graduated taxation of slaves and financial incentives to
free them (in 1860, the Virginia legislature came within one vote
of abolishing slavery in Virginia), unions were a percipitate, ill-
conceived 'solution' that did vast damage and left a continuing
trail of waste and corruption.  Unions are unhealthy by nature.  I
don't mean the right of free people to form unions, that is a given.
I mean the use they are created for.  A free person should not have
the right to coerce another free person to their will.  Nor should
a group of people have such right.  What is the difference in a
union saying "we will strike and harrass and ruin your company if
you don't give us such-and-such" and an extortioner saying "I will
bomb and ruin your company if you don't do such-and-such?"  The
answer is nothing, they are the same.  "I will hurt you if you
don't bow to my demands."  That whole concept is wrong.  We should
give such power only to the government, where it can be checked by
law, and by the will of the citizens through due process.  I have
no problem with employees forming a union and going to management
to say "We are unhappy, and unless you comply with such-and-such
conditions, we will quit."  Fine, that's simple negotiation.  But
if they do quit, they have no business picketing or harrassing the
company.  Those are nothing more than legalized extortion.

> Let me also point out that while our job requires both technical
> skill, ability, and pure raw talent to do, "management" would like
> nothing better than to be able to reduce 'Programmers' to
> $5.45/hour type positions.

True, but that's what free market is all about.  MacDonald's
would no doubt love to sell their hamburgers for ten times the
current price, but they won't get away with it as long as Burger
King, et al are around. :-)

> Check out the latest round of silver bullet disease for
> confirmation - once again they are on the kick that
> you can buy a product (like say, VA/Generator) and
> have cheap programmers use it effectively without
> having to teach them how to program. (*sigh*)

Just because they want to do that doesn't mean they can make it work,
does it?  But if they can, then they have a point, don't they? :-)
```

```
Judson McClendon wrote:

> > We don't "give" that power to anyone.  The government takes it because it
> > can.  Unions take it because they (sometimes) can.  "Due process" does
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> permit it, even if by our inaction and inattention.

That doesn't happen, but politicians do make unpopular decisions.  I watched
PARADE the other day, where a 1915? governor  of Georgia commuted the sentence
of someone who was accused of rape.  This guy was a northern Jew and the people
wanted to hang him.  And they did lynch him and the governor did flee days
before the end of his term (in real life).

Still, if they could, they would make unpopular decisions quietly.

If you gain power by gun point, by voodoo, by compromise, or whether you gain
power by being as charismatic as Hitler, you still have power.  And if I agree
with what those in power do, I am lucky.  If not, too bad.

One reason unions are not popular with computer professionals, is that computer
professionals have enough power to make their own choices.  We generally don't
believe that giving up power to the unions will be more useful than keeping
it.  We believe that we should be able to negotiate with prospective employers
instead of accepting what others decided (there are exceptions - I am currently
working for the state!!).  We believe that changing jobs will pay off better
than gaining seniority.  If there was only one job in town, with low entrance
requirements - our outlook would be different.  The power in town (the
corporation) would likely need a counter power (a union).  I like having the
power spread out without anybody having absolute control.
```

```
From another forum, we have the effects of employees quitting en
masse. As anybody ever heard of quality and its cost?

JB

"Gov. Bush values the work of state employees and his staff is
concerned about our turnover rate," Jones (Mike Jones, a spokesman for
Gov. George W. Bush) said. 

"If the governor is in office next year, he will look forward to
working with legislative leaders on ideas to retain our best public
servants."

Billy Hamilton, state deputy comptroller, said turnover is causing a
"hollowing-out of the work force." The gap between the newest
employees and the senior workers means those with six to 15 years of
experience are absent.

"I think there's going to be some hard times ahead," Hamilton said.

Although the transportation department only saw a 9.8 percent turnover
rate in 1999, Jarratt said 25 of 30 people who were in his group in
1993 are gone.

He said new employees are asking for changes that they believe save
money but actually leave bumps that his company must pave. 

Then cars and trucks bounce over the road, hammering it to pieces.

"The product they are going to be putting out is not going to be the
same quality Texas DOT has put out over the last 83 years," he said.

Much of the surplus was returned to taxpayers in the form of a tax
cut, rather than being plowed back into maintaining services,
Armbrister said.

"Joe Sixpack wants the money back," he said.

Gary W. Anderson, executive director of the Texas Public Employees
Association, said paying to retain veteran workers is a matter of
priorities.

"What the people of Texas have come to expect will be gone," he said. 

"Once the infrastructure is gone, it may be very expensive to rebuild
it."





From The Fort Worth Star-Telegram, 9/25/00:    
http://www.star-telegram.com/news/doc/1047/1:STATE12/1:STATE120924100.html

 
State employees leaving in droves, cost Texas $250 million 

By CHRIS ROBERTS
Associated Press 

EL PASO, Texas -- 

State employees repair roads, guard prison inmates, administer welfare
and maintain computer systems. 

They oversee agricultural programs, fight wildfires and patrol the
state's highways.

And in 1999, 17.6 percent of them -- 25,421 people -- left the system.

That's slightly higher than the 1998 rate of 17.4 percent and above
the national average of 14.9 percent.

The result is that lost productivity and constant training are costing
the state between $127 million and $254 million each year, according
to a March report from the state auditor's office.
```

```
On Wed, 27 Sep 2000 06:24:55 GMT, an orbiting mind control laser caused Liam
Devlin <liamddd@optonline.net> to write:

>> >There is a disease, fairly common in USA, that bleeding will treat.
>> 
…[7 more quoted lines elided]…
>informational meeting I missed.

I forget the formal latin name, but the condition causes these people to store
up iron, which then can reach toxic levels.  Some people with this condition
can store up so much iron they set off airport metal detectors nude.

The simplest and most effective treatment for this condition is bloodletting
as noted above.  About 5-7 years ago there was a stink that didn't make
national news about the situation, as described above.  I am told that the
rules have changed -- unfortunately after the Red Cross came though the office
so I have no way to verify.  But every time I hear the radio & TV say the Red
Cross is crying about blood reserves I call and tell them about this, but for
some reason that side of the story is always suppressed.

The problem being that blood donation is not covered under medical insurance,
even though it is medically necessary with these people, but the Red Cross
wouldn't take blood from people with this condition.  At one point there was
documented proof of Red Cross officials lobbying the FDA to prevent the use of
such blood in transfusions, at which point I stopped donating to the Red Cross
(blood and money).  My disaster relief donations now go to the Salvation Army,
which does a better job off the starting line.
```

```
It seems Jimmy Hoffa isn't dead.  He really left his mark.  One would think
all union leaders were just like him, rather than he being an icon because
he was so far-and-away atypical.  Not to say there is no union corruption,
just that it is no more common that corruption in other forms of governance.

I'd also note, it seems few of you have read much by Charles Dickens.  You
have no idea of the tradition of management oppression of workers.  The game
is to get as much out of the worker for as little input as possible.
Something called efficiency.

"Judson McClendon" <judmc@bellsouth.net> wrote in message
news:sALz5.2564$103.17378@news1.atl...
> "Paul Raulerson" <praul@cegi.no-spam-net> wrote:
<<snip>>
> Unions are unhealthy by nature.  I
> don't mean the right of free people to form unions, that is a given.
> I mean the use they are created for.  A free person should not have
> the right to coerce another free person to their will.

So you aren't in managment then.  I've had the course and I've done the job.

Delegation, encouragement, motivation, persuasion, co-opting, coercion,
discipline and sacking are popular topics.  You start with the first, but
have to be prepared to move to the end if need be.  Of course one tries to
avoid humiliation.

>Nor should
> a group of people have such right.  What is the difference in a
> union saying "we will strike and harrass and ruin your company if
> you don't give us such-and-such" and an extortioner saying "I will
> bomb and ruin your company if you don't do such-and-such?"

Some countries bomb each other for no good reason.  That doesn't mean all
countries bomb each other for no good reason.

There are a few examples of unions destroying their employers.  Most of
these in Britain under the Labour government back 20 to 40 years ago.

There have been bitter labor battles in the US, but GM is still around, Ford
is still around.  Chrysler is part of another company.

There have been other bitter labor battles, like the one for migrant
workers, but then they had reason to fight.

There have been industries that have died out, but usually due to
mismanagement.

> answer is nothing, they are the same.  "I will hurt you if you
> don't bow to my demands."  That whole concept is wrong.  We should
> give such power only to the government, where it can be checked by
> law, and by the will of the citizens through due process.

A democratic government is just a bunch of elected officials.  A
contemporary union is just another type of democratic government.

>I have
> no problem with employees forming a union and going to management
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> King, et al are around. :-)

If we aren't worth the money, we aren't going to get the pay.  I agree it is
as simple as that.

But we are worth the money.  There are tremendous differences in the talents
of programmers.  Not the order of magnituide difference in productivity
found in old studies.

There are major differences in the efficiency, operability, realiability and
modifiability of code from one programmer to another.  Many programmers and
many analyst produce cheap junk.  Some produce great stuff.

>
> > Check out the latest round of silver bullet disease for
…[6 more quoted lines elided]…
> does it?  But if they can, then they have a point, don't they? :-)

Agreed.

> --
> Judson McClendon      judmc@bellsouth.net
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
```

- **Date:** 2000-09-25T00:00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.software.year-2000,alt.computer.consultants,comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<39CF96BC.5D80A5AE@brazee.net>`
- **References:** `<enODdtoo9NcB-pn2-IHlsjbuDKmC5@localhost> <8q5rf7$7sq$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <0Awx5.794$D83.53886@nnrp3.sbc.net> <39C6A322.DFAF296@blarney.att.net> <39C75CF5.BD7D151E@bellatlantic.net> <39C76BAF.E831ECD4@brazee.net> <39C84CD0.59970393@dio.com> <39C5ECB6.2BCDDEC9@kg.hsanet.net> <39CAFB14.E7DD4E23@optonline.net> <8qg2qd$965$1@nnrp1.deja.com> <BPsz5.358$583.32719@news1.onlynews.com> <39CEE603.89F9513@MtPellerin.au> <8qo28q$vrr$1@nnrp1.deja.com>`

```
philotsopher wrote:

> Your employer's interest is to make as much money as possible and to
> pay you as little as possible. You are very confused.

How much it pays you is not it's direct interest.  It's interest is to make
as much money as possible.  If it achieves this result by paying you very
little, then those both will be true.  If it achieves this by paying you
more and getting a better return, then it will do that.

Now, there are some other goals an employer has as well.  Apparently CEO's
make more money for being big than for being profitable.  Bill Gates would
rather have the power of being the head of a united Microsoft than to be
even richer with by owning most of two Microsofts (look at the net value of
Standard Oil & the phone company after breakups and compare it to IBM which
succeeded in staying together).

But keeping your pay as low as possible is like you keeping the cost your
tools as low as possible.  Yes it's nice to save money, and the money saved
can be used to further its real goal - but having the right tool at the
right time is also useful in furthering its real goal.

"The People" only get and maintain power if they get and maintain value to
those in power.  (If you get power via revolution, you trade leaders - that
isn't sufficient) To help "The People", make them valuable.
```

```
physicians.  It works for airline pilots.

Where unions do not work is where the labor is easily replacable, or where
the company is easily replaceable.  That is why unions do not work well for

Or the banks.  Most groups in society have organized representation of some
kind.  Pick one you like that is doing a good job, and emulate them.

There is some sector of American life you like isn't there?
```

```
> you lower salaries due to lower demand.

   Nope, you don't even understand basic supply and demand. If you have a
shortage then increasing quantitied supplied works to lessen the shortage.
If prices are rigid downward, such as in a situation where suppliers will
not accept lower prices, then what happens as supply increases is that the
shortage is lessened but prices don't fall. The fact that prices (in this
case salaries) are still going up, it seems pretty certain that there is no
"glut" of labor.

remain unimproved by such a redistribution of income. It is socialist myth
```

```


"P.W. Goss" wrote:

> On Fri, 22 Sep 2000 16:58:39 GMT, in comp.software.year-2000 you
> wrote:
…[25 more quoted lines elided]…
> there on the wall.  You merely need to take time to read it.

  You are SO right! I have left firms that I could see were about to
undergo a huge bloodletting and after I left, sure enough they did. It is
never the case that a firm is making shitloads of money, being
well-accepted in the market, growing without losing profitability and then
in the middle of all this just starts cutting blindly clear out of the
blue. It is always the case that they are suffering losses, seeing
problems with their marketshare, or have some kind of huge lawsuit pending
against them that everyone knows about.

   A few years ago I could figure out rather easily that my then-employer
was not making any money and was burning through VC money at very high
rates. It was easy to see that the number of orders and their size was
simply way too small and wasn't growing. Another sign was that the firm
was being severely cannibalized by a consulting firm and yet was
unbelievably cheap to the point where they KNEW it was cutting into their
ability to produce. I knew that VC's weren't going to let that last
forever and would soon start requiring cost-cutting or would withdraw
their money. I left. Sure enough, three months later the firm laid off 40%
of their staff and most of those layoffs were the good, competent but more
expensive employees who had been there the longest and had been the most
faithful. I knew that the management there was going to react exactly in
that fashion when the time of reckoning occured.
```

```
A very good question. Liberal (as in left-leaning) governments want to
supply as much assistance as possible. What these governments CAN
provide is education. What these governments CANNOT provide is
wealth-creating jobs. (Governments cannot produce wealth - governments
can only destroy wealth.)

Left-leaning (as in liberal) governments believe that wealth can be
neither created nor destroyed, that the amount of wealth is fixed.
These same governments do believe, however, that the available wealth
can be redistributed. So, they take what wealth they have, train their
citizens, their citizens come to the USA and thereby redistribute the
wealth. Makes sense to me.
```

```
irrelevant to the outcome, even if you vote for the winner. Unless you are

candidate, not only is your individual vote irrelevant to the outcome, so
is your opinion (as is mine). Even though I think you are an idiot, I
could care less if you vote or who you vote for, even if it were the
socialist candidate. We are both irrelevant to the presidential election.

   That's the strange thing about democracy, it sedates the masses by
granting them the illusion of individual power. Mainitaining that illusion
protects us from anarchy, totalitarianism and despotism. Odd.
```
